    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e8f2809f66ce471a83475bc149bd56c0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd9427390-3fdf-4da7-96a9-24dc06839da6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Guangzhou Key Laboratory for Surface Chemistry of Energy Materials, New Energy Research Institute, School of Environment and Energy, South China University of Technology, Guangzhou, China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Liu/Yuqiao">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Deng/Qiang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Qiang Deng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">
                     <p class="author-name">Qiang Deng</p>
                     <p>Guangzhou Key Laboratory for Surface Chemistry of Energy Materials, New Energy Research Institute, School of Environment and Energy, South China University of Technology, Guangzhou, China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Deng/Qiang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Cheng/Qian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a5" data-db-target-for="a5" aria-controls="a5" aria-haspopup="true" id="a5_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Qian Cheng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a5" aria-labelledby="a5_Ctrl" role="region" id="a5">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d76722597" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>For the performance optimization strategies of hard carbon, heteroatom doping is an effective way to enhance the intrinsic transfer properties of sodium ions and electrons for accelerating the reaction kinetics. However, the previous work focuses mainly on the intrinsic physicochemical property changes of the material, but little attention has been paid to the resulting interfacial regulation of the electrode surface, namely the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. In this work, element F, which has the highest electronegativity, was chosen as the doping source to, more effectively, tune the electronic structure of the hard carbon. The effect of F-doping on the physicochemical properties of hard carbon was not only systematically analyzed but also investigated with spectroscopy, optics, and in situ characterization techniques to further verify that appropriate F-doping plays a positive role in constructing a homogenous and inorganic-rich SEI film. The experimentally demonstrated link between the electronic structure of the electrode and the SEI film properties can reframe the doping optimization strategy as well as provide a new idea for the design of electrode materials with low reduction kinetics to the electrolyte. As a result, the optimized sample with the appropriate F-doping content exhibits the best electrochemical performance with high capacity (434.53 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and excellent rate capability (141 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>).</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2503-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2503-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>The development of energy storage battery technology is the key to achieving high-efficiency applications of clean energy.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0001_R_d76722569e344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew Chem Int Ed">1</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0002_R_d76722569e344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv Energy Mater">2</a></sup></span> At present, the industrialization of alkali metal-ion batteries is largely limited by the anode performance. From the aspect of improving the capacity of the batteries, traditional high-performance Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode exhibits marvelous theoretical capacity and is typically combined with carbon-based materials (like carbon dots) to address issues such as poor conductivity and low initial Coulombic efficiency.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0003_R_d76722569e351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano-Micro Lett">3</a></sup></span> However, considering the possibility of large-scale application, hard carbon has extensive resources, adjustable physicochemical properties, and low oxidation/reduction potential, making it the most promising anode material, especially for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0004_R_d76722569e354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv Energy Mater">4</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0005_R_d76722569e354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ Sci">5</a></sup></span> Compared with traditional graphite, sodium ions can form more stable intercalation compounds in the graphitic microcrystallite layers of hard carbon without thermodynamic problems. Moreover, the nanopores composed of distorted graphitic microcrystallites can also provide more space for the aggregation of quasi-metallic sodium clusters. These synergistic effects can contribute more than 35 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (for graphite) of sodium storage capacity to hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0006_R_d76722569e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv Energy Mater">6</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0007_R_d76722569e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Proc Natl Acad Sci USA">7</a></sup></span> However, with the rapid development of large-scale energy storage fields, the limited active sites and slow intercalation reaction kinetics result in a hard carbon anode unable to meet the higher energy density requirements. In recent years, researchers have proposed various optimization strategies, including nanoscale design, hierarchical porous structure regulation, and heteroatom doping modification, which have significantly improved the electrochemical performance of hard carbon.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0008_R_d76722569e363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Acc Chem Res">8</a>-<a href="#cey2503-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0011_R_d76722569e363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J Mater Chem A">11</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>As one of the most direct and effective functionalization modification methods, the influence of heteroatom on electrochemical performance is mainly related to the doping form. Some heteroatoms exist in the form of surface functional groups, which can be used either as nucleation/anchoring sites for electrolyte adsorption/desorption or as electrochemical redox reaction active sites, significantly improving sodium storage capacity. The other part of the heteroatoms occupies the carbon sites in the lattice, which can not only expand the graphitic microcrystallite layer spacing but also adjust the electronic structure of carbon, thus enhancing the diffusion dynamics.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0012_R_d76722569e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ Sci">12</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0013_R_d76722569e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv Energy Mater">13</a></sup></span> These factors can effectively enhance the electrochemical performance of hard carbon, including specific capacity, cycling life, and rate performance. N is one of the most common doped atoms, and the corresponding dopants such as NH<sub>4</sub>Cl, (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and urea.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0004_R_d76722569e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv Energy Mater">4</a></sup></span> Khan et al. synthesized N-doped carbon spheres using urea as an&nbsp;N source.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0014_R_d76722569e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Solid State Ion">14</a></sup></span> Under the effect of increasing defects and expanding layer spacing, the reversible specific capacity of carbon spheres increased from 129.5 to 225.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;and could remain stable for 2000 cycles at a high rate. Moreover, Hong et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0015_R_d76722569e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Energy">15</a></sup></span> designed hollow nanostructured N-doped carbon by the tailoring of carbon quantum dots for the first time. The high-resolution N 1s core level X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectrum shows that the fitted peaks at approximately 398.3, 400.6, and 403.9 eV are attributed to pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, and graphitic N, which are three typical configuration types for doped N. By the theoretical calculations, Sun et al. explored the differences between these configurations.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0016_R_d76722569e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J Energy Chem">16</a></sup></span> The introduction of graphitic N caused the Fermi level shift to the conduction band, which may be related to the extra electrons brought in. The electronic density of the state near the Fermi level is improved, indicating that the conductivity of the material is increased, thereby improving the transfer kinetics of the sodium ion and electron. Different from graphitic N, the doping of pyridinic N and pyrrolic N can produce defects, more extra holes will be dominated, and the Fermi level will shift to the valance band. The electrons will accumulate at the pyridinic N and pyrrolic N sites to form the negative electron's center, resulting in lower adsorption energy for sodium ions. In addition, the binding energy between sodium ion and pyridinic N is stronger than that between sodium ion and pyrrolic N, which is more conducive to the adsorption of sodium ions. S is an electroactive active element that can also provide the extra electrochemical active site and expand graphitic microcrystallite layer spacing. In fact, the effect of S atoms from different dopants on hard carbon is also different.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0017_R_d76722569e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv Mater">17</a></sup></span> The S-doped hard carbon synthesized from S powder contains more of the graphitic domain structure, and the interlayer spacing between graphitic microcrystallite layers reaches 3.95 Å, indicating that S atoms enter the carbon layer and form C–S–C bonds. When Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is used as an S source, the hard carbon has a layer spacing of 3.65 Å and a more abundant pore structure, which showed in-plane doping of S atoms at defect sites in the graphitic microcrystallite layers. When the interlayer spacing of graphitic microcrystallite increased from 3.5 to 3.9 Å, the corresponding barrier energy decreased from 392 to 164 meV, indicating that S-doping can improve the diffusion difficulty of sodium ions. Moreover, during the charging/discharging process, the reversible changes of the characteristic peaks corresponding to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (XPS) and the characteristic peaks representing C–S bonds and S–S bonds (Raman spectra) confirm that the reaction of S with sodium involves the cleavage and reconstruction of the C–S<sub><i>x</i></sub>–C (<i>x</i> = 1, 2) bond.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0018_R_d76722569e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ Sci">18</a></sup></span> Recently, Cai et al.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0019_R_d76722569e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv Energy Mater">19</a></sup></span> successfully implemented sulfur-assisted synthetic strategies by the structural design of interlayered/in-plane C–S bridge bonds, achieving breakthroughs in large capacities and fast kinetics. The intrinsic processes (formation mechanism of interlayered/in-plane C–S bridge bonds) between sulfur and nature biomass precursors were determined using the density functional theory calculations, and the routes of interlayered/in-plane C–S bridge bonds were confirmed. Commonly used doped atoms include O, P, and B.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0020_R_d76722569e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Prog Energy Combust Sci">20</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>F is a halogen element with the highest electronegativity (3.98) and exhibits the electron-withdrawing effect. In theory, F can produce the highest positive charge on carbon atoms and is also a usable doped atom. At present, F-doped carbon-based materials are mainly graphite, and there are few reports on the modification of hard carbon anodes for SIBs. Herein, a series of fluorine-doped hard carbon (denoted as FHCT<sub><i>x</i></sub>, T means tetrafluoroterephthalic acid, C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>2</sub>F<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>; <i>x</i> represents the mass ratio of C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>2</sub>F<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>: pristine HC) with adjustable F content and graphitization degree is synthesized by simple heat treatment. The influence of F-doping on the physicochemical properties of hard carbon was systematically analyzed, and the differences in electrochemical performance were compared. The correlation between F-doping and electrochemical reactions was constructed by theoretical calculations. Meanwhile, various in-situ analysis techniques were used to analyze the sodium storage mechanism. It is worth noting that F-doping leads to hard-carbon electronic-structure changes that have a regulatory effect on the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film in SIBs, which has not been previously reported to our knowledge.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2503-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2503-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2503-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2503-sec-0030-title">2.1 Microstructure and surface element state</h3>
            
            <p>The microscopic morphology and structure of all the samples are studied by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As the SEM images in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0001">1A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> shown, all samples exhibit a flat platelet shape with a particle diameter size of approximately 15 μm, indicating that F-doping did not change the original morphology. From the TEM image of FHCT<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0001">1B</a>), the morphological characteristics of single particles are almost consistent with the results of SEM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2503-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cf554b1-0a00-4d1c-a5b5-5bfc7c67aeb2/cey2503-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cf554b1-0a00-4d1c-a5b5-5bfc7c67aeb2/cey2503-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0626636c-0854-4721-af3e-97f64894870e/cey2503-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cf554b1-0a00-4d1c-a5b5-5bfc7c67aeb2/cey2503-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2503-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) SEM images of PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>. (B) TEM images of FHCT<sub>2</sub>. (C) HRTEM images of PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3</sub>, and the corresponding layer spacing. (D) EDS elemental mapping of FHCT<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The graphitic microcrystallite region of the hard carbon material is the key to the insertion/extraction of sodium ions. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) was applied to analyze the microstructure of the samples. Note that all samples have the typical characteristics of hard carbon, namely the existence of local order and long-range disorder graphitic microcrystallite regions and a large number of micro/mesopores, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0001">1C</a>. In addition, the carbon layer spacing for PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2,</sub> and FHCT<sub>3</sub> are 0.371, 0.388, 0.390, and 0.397 nm, respectively, revealing that the layer spacing gradually increases with the increase of the F-doping amount, which could tolerate more sodium ions and better accommodate volume expansion. The uniform distributions of C, O, and F elements within the FHCT<sub>2</sub> are also verified by energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) element mappings in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0001">1D</a>, indicating&nbsp;the successful doping of F.</p>
            
            <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns show two broad diffraction peaks at around 23°&nbsp;and 44°, corresponding to the (002) and (101) crystal planes in the carbon structure, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2A</a>), which are consistent with the non-graphitic nature of hard carbon. Based on the Bragg equation, the carbon layer spacing can be calculated as 0.373 nm for PHC, 0.384 nm for FHCT<sub>1</sub>, 0.392 nm for FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and 0.399 nm for FHCT<sub>3</sub>; that is, F-doping can expand the spacing of graphitic microcrystallite layers, which is coincident with the results of HRTEM. The domain thickness along the c-axis of a graphitic structure can be calculated by the Debye–Scherrer equation for PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3,</sub> where there are, on average, 6.45, 6.06, 5.78, and 5.51 layers of graphene sheets, respectively (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). We also calculated empirical <i>R</i> values based on the ratio of the intensity of the (002) peak to the background of the equivalent peak, with <i>R</i> values of 1.614 and 1.575 for PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The calculation details can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>. A higher <i>R</i> value indicates a higher degree of graphitization. The above results show a decrease in graphitization after F-doping; that is, the disorder increases.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2503-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a52d15e8-87b3-4244-bafd-7f413830af9d/cey2503-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a52d15e8-87b3-4244-bafd-7f413830af9d/cey2503-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a5353cb1-3c3b-4e69-97f7-c6549d7f21be/cey2503-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a52d15e8-87b3-4244-bafd-7f413830af9d/cey2503-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2503-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) XRD patterns of PHC and FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub>. (B) Raman spectra of PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>. (C) The fluorine content of PHC and FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> &nbsp;based on XPS measurements. (D) Curve fitting for XPS F 1s spectra of all samples. (E) High-resolution C 1s spectra of PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>. (F) High-resolution O 1s spectra of PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The structure and graphitization were investigated by Raman spectra (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2B</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). To minimize the fitting error, the Raman spectrum was deconvoluted into four peaks<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0021_R_d76722569e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Carbon">21</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0022_R_d76722569e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Carbon">22</a></sup></span>: the G band at 1594 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from the in-plane C–C bond stretching of sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized graphitic carbon atoms (E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry); the D1 band at 1341 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from the vibrations of disordered carbon atoms at graphite sheet edges or a defective graphitic structure (A<sub>1g</sub> symmetry); the D3 band at 1500 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from the short-range sp<sup>3</sup> carbon vibrations of amorphous carbon; the D4 band at 1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from the disordered graphitic lattice. In general, the area ratio under the D3 and G bands (I<sub>D3</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) can be considered as an indicator of the disorder degree. The intensity ratios of I<sub>D3</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> for PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2,</sub> and FHCT<sub>3</sub> are 0.774, 0.969, 1.008, and 1.351, respectively, further confirming that more F doping amount contributes to an increase in the degree of disorder of the samples. The high degree of disorder originates from defects caused by F-doping, which may provide additional sodium-ion storage sites.</p>
            
            <p>XPS was applied to research the bond configuration of various elements on the surface of all materials. First, the high-resolution F 1s spectra of FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> (<i>X</i> = 1, 2, 3) except PHC all exhibit obvious peaks at 687–689 eV, corresponding to the C–F bond, and the peak intensity enhances with the increase of F-doping amount (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2C</a>), verifying that F atoms dope into the hard carbon successfully. Specifically, 687.3 and 688.5 eV are associated with semi-ionic C–F bonds and covalent C–F bonds. Previous work reports that C–F bonds tend to be semi-ionic bonds in softly fluorinated graphene and to be covalent bonds in highly fluorinated graphene.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0023_R_d76722569e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv Sci">23</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2D</a>, with increasing F content, the binding energy of C–F bond showed a slight rightward shift, indicating more covalent C–F bond content and less semi-ionic C–F bond content. Higher content of covalent C–F will cause lower electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0024_R_d76722569e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - RSC Adv">24</a></sup></span> Four-point probe method was used to measure the conductivity of PHC (18.01 S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and FHCT<sub>2</sub> (13.58 S cm<sup>−1</sup>), verifying that F-doping modulates the electronic structure of hard carbon. In addition, for the C 1s spectra, all samples contain three peaks located at 284.6, 286.0, and 287.4 eV, corresponding to C–C/C═C, C–O, and C═O bonds, respectively. In contrast, FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> exhibits an additional characteristic peak belonging to the C–F bond at 288.8 eV (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2E</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). The O 1s spectra of all samples contain three peaks at 531.32, 532.15 and 533.37 eV, which could be attributed to the C(O)O, C═O and C–O bands, respectively (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0002">2F</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0025_R_d76722569e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Carbon">25</a>, <a href="#cey2503-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0026_R_d76722569e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem Eng J">26</a></sup></span> Oxygen functional groups also play a vital role in hard carbon as they provide extra Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites via the following process: –C═O + Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> → –C–O–Na.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0027_R_d76722569e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Sustainable Chem Eng">27</a></sup></span> Collectively, the adjustable synthesis of FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> with various microstructures and homogenous F-doping is in target realized.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2503-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2503-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical performance measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance was evaluated in half coin cells using Na metal as the counter electrode. Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> show the cyclic voltammetry (CV) of the initial three cycles for PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2,</sub> and FHCT<sub>3</sub> electrodes in a voltage window of 0.001–2.5 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Compared with the F-doped samples, PHC features a varied peak shape; that is, the potential difference of the pair of redox peaks is larger, leading to a larger overpotential. This result confirms that F-doping can improve electrochemical reaction kinetics. Besides, PHC and FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> (<i>X</i> = 1, 2, 3) samples all show obvious broad SEI peaks around 0.3–1.0 V in the first discharge process, which disappeared in subsequent cycles because of the formation of SEI. In contrast, PHC possesses a higher reduction peak potential than the F-doped materials, indicating that the electrolyte on the surface of the PHC electrode starts to decompose earlier during the first discharge. Notably, in addition to a pair of sharp reversible redox peaks (located near 0.01 V) indicating the insertion/extraction behavior of sodium ions between graphite-like layers, a pair of broad reversible redox peaks located at about 0.50 V appeared in the second and third CV curves for both FHCT<sub><i>X</i></sub> (<i>X</i> = 1, 2, 3) and PHC samples, as shown in the red box mark in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3A</a>. It corresponds to the presence of the dual potential plateaus of the charge curves, reflecting the existence of two different reaction mechanisms. It has been reported that the high-potential plateau region (0.50–0.80 V) is associated with the surface sodiation process of oxygen-containing functional groups, which can be confirmed by the XPS results.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0028_R_d76722569e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv Mater">28</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2503-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/84ac57a4-03aa-4266-91c2-5c5e01749f92/cey2503-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/84ac57a4-03aa-4266-91c2-5c5e01749f92/cey2503-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b46a0521-af8d-4d41-aaaa-ec9482a8b188/cey2503-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/84ac57a4-03aa-4266-91c2-5c5e01749f92/cey2503-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2503-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.503" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization of different electrodes. (A) The first 3 consecutive CV curves of PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>. (B) The first charge/discharge curves of PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3</sub> at 20 mA g <sup>−1</sup>. (C) Comparison of capacity above and below 0.1 V of the first cycle in different electrode systems. (D) The cycling performance of PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3</sub> at 100 mA g <sup>−1</sup>. (E) Rate performance of PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3</sub> from 20 to 400 mA g <sup>−1</sup>. (F) Long-term cycling performance of FHCT<sub>2</sub> at 400 mA g <sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The galvanostatic discharge–charge curves measured at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> show that all electrodes display similar voltage profiles, which can be divided into a sloping region (2.5–0.1 V) and a plateau region (below 0.1 V) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3B</a>). The initial reversible capacity and Coulombic efficiency are 379.27 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, 85.67%, 394.33 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, 86.02%, 434.53 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, 85.89%, and 354.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, 82.31% for PHC, FHCT<sub>1</sub>, FHCT<sub>2</sub>, and FHCT<sub>3</sub>, respectively. From the calculated capacity contribution ratio (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3C</a>), it is evident that the majority of the capacity comes from the plateau region. The cycling performance and corresponding Coulombic efficiency of all anodes for 100 charge/discharge cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3D</a>. The FHCT<sub>2</sub> anode also exhibits the highest reversible capacity of 315.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, and the Coulombic efficiency exceeds 98% after the first cycle, representing excellent cyclic stability and reversibility. However, the cycling performances of FHCT<sub>1</sub>, PHC, and FHCT<sub>3</sub> deteriorated sequentially, indicating that excessive F-doping would bring side effects. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3E</a>, the rate capabilities of all samples were measured and compared at various current densities from 20 to 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The FHCT<sub>2</sub> anode displays highest rate capacities of 452.3, 402.9, 347.1, 264.1, and 141 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20, 40, 100, 200, and 400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Based on the excellent electrochemical performance, the FHCT<sub>2</sub> sample can also operate stably for 1000 cycles at high current density (400 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), with a Coulombic efficiency of almost 100% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0003">3F</a>).</p>
            
            <p>In addition, we further investigated the kinetic effects of F-doping on sodium-ion diffusion and the formation process of SEI film by in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests during the first cycle (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0029_R_d76722569e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Electrochim Acta">29</a></sup></span> In the initial state, the Nyquist curves are composed of a semicircle (medium-high-frequency range) and a slanted line (low-frequency range). The first point on the real axis corresponds to the ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), the semicircle is related to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) between the electrode and electrolyte, and the diagonal represents the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) caused by sodium ions during diffusion.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0030_R_d76722569e877" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - RSC Adv">30</a></sup></span> As the discharge depth intensifies, a new semicircle appears in the Nyquist curves in the high-frequency range, which belongs to the diffusion resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) of sodium ions in the formed SEI film. For the PHC electrode, the start of SEI film formation was observed at 0.8 V, which was higher than that of the FHCT<sub>2</sub> electrode at 0.6 V, indicating that the electrolyte reduction and SEI film formation on the FHCT<sub>2</sub> electrode were hindered, which may be related to F-doping. Compared with the PHC electrode, the lower <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> value indicates faster transport of sodium ions in FHCT<sub>2</sub>-derived SEI film, which is responsible for the improved rate performance. Furthermore, the FHCT<sub>2</sub> electrode shows a smaller total resistance than the PHC electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4A</a>), meaning its lower interfacial resistance and lower overpotential during operation.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0031_R_d76722569e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl Mater Interfaces">31</a></sup></span> F-doping with defects as active sites can kinetically favor the electrochemical process with faster electronic and sodium-ion transport, leading to superior sodium-ion storage capability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2503-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/883750e4-f38b-43ec-a53a-226bea507981/cey2503-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2503-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2503-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium storage mechanisms</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests are applied to analyze the sodium storage behaviors in different potential ranges by investigating the diffusion coefficient of the sodium ion (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) in PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub>. We used a pulse current of 0.02 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 0.5 h, accompanied by a rest interval of 2.0 h. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4B</a>, the similar shape of the GITT curves indicates that the two samples possess a similar sodium storage mechanism. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was computed according to Fick's second law (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#cey2503-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0032_R_d76722569e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Carbon Energy">32</a></sup></span>:

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0003" display="block" wiley:location="equation/cey2503-math-0003.png" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;D&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi mathvariant="italic"&gt;Na&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x0003D}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mfrac&gt;&lt;mn&gt;4&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mi&gt;\unicode{x003C0}&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000B7}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mi&gt;\unicode{x003C4}&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mfrac&gt;&lt;msup&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000B7}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mfenced&gt;&lt;mfrac&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;m&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi&gt;B&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000B7}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mi&gt;V&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;mi&gt;m&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;M&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi&gt;B&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000B7}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mi&gt;A&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mfrac&gt;&lt;/mfenced&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/msup&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x000B7}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mspace width="0.15em"/&gt;&lt;msup&gt;&lt;mfenced&gt;&lt;mfrac&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x02206}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;E&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi&gt;S&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x02206}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;E&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi&gt;\unicode{x003C4}&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mfrac&gt;&lt;/mfenced&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/msup&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mo&gt;(&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mi&gt;\unicode{x003C4}&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x0226A}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msup&gt;&lt;mi&gt;L&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/msup&gt;&lt;mo&gt;/&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;D&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mi&gt;Na&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;mo&gt;)&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>τ</i> (s), <i>m<sub>B</sub></i> (g), <i>V<sub>m</sub></i> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup>), <i>M<sub>B</sub></i> (g mol<sup>−1</sup>), and <i>A</i> (m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) are the pulse duration, active mass of the electrode, molar volume, molecular weight, and active surface area of the electrode, respectively. Additionally, ∆<i>E<sub>S</sub></i> and ∆<i>E<sub>τ</sub></i> are obtained from the GITT curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4C</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0033_R_d76722569e1108" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater">33</a></sup></span> The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> is shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4D,E</a>. The accessibility for the sodium ion of active sites on the hard carbon surface is higher compared with the interlayer space in the graphitic microcrystallite region. Thus, the slope region responding to the adsorption capacity has a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>, which can be attributed to the sodium-ion storage on the surface defects. For the plateau in the 0.001–0.10 V range, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of discharge suffered a plunge and rebound. Generally, when sodium-ion insertion commences, the sodium ion must overcome the repulsive charge gradient from the previously combined sodium-ion at the superficial sites; hence, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> would fall. A rebound of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> before reaching the cut-off potential responds to the occurrence of a new sodium storage mechanism. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> shows a rising trend indicating a gradual weakening of binding energy close to the sodium metal potential, consistent with the process of sodium plating. It suggests that the filling in closed micro/mesopores occurs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0034_R_d76722569e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl Energy Mater">34</a></sup></span> Based on the evolution of the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>, we make reasonable speculation on the sodium storage mechanism of the low-potential plateau region: sodium ion first inserted into graphitic layers and later filled into micro/mesopores. Notably, an extra high-potential plateau appears in the 0.5–0.8 V range, corresponding to a variation of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> at around 0.6 V within the high-potential region (&gt;0.1 V).<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0028_R_d76722569e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv Mater">28</a></sup></span> Combined with the analysis of CV results, this high-potential plateau is mainly attributed to the sodium storage active sites provided by the oxygen-containing functional groups.</div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium storage mechanism for PHC and FHCT<sub>2</sub> was also investigated by CV at the scan rates varying in the 0.1–1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> range (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4F</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8A</a>). The peak current obeys a power-law relationship with the scan rate according to the followoing equation<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0035_R_d76722569e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Storage Mater">35</a></sup></span>:

                  </semantics></math> capacitive process (proportional to <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a19f0142-093f-4e1c-9c9c-846db704b1f5/cey2503-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0013" location="graphic/cey2503-math-0013.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0013" wiley:location="equation/cey2503-math-0013.png" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mi&gt;v&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) and a diffusion-controlled process (proportional to <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/23add154-6bff-47a8-b774-ae38d61456d0/cey2503-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0014" location="graphic/cey2503-math-0014.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mrow>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0014" wiley:location="equation/cey2503-math-0014.png" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;msup&gt;&lt;mi&gt;v&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mn&gt;1&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mo&gt;/&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/msup&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>), the capacitance contribution can be quantitatively distinguished by the following equation<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0037_R_d76722569e1282" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv Sci">37</a></sup></span>:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="cey2503-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/28ba1179-fafe-430e-8f92-e109426be3be/cey2503-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0015" display="block" location="graphic/cey2503-math-0015.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mo>=</mo>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>k</mi>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                 </msub>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi>k</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msub>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>v</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mn>1</mn>
                                       <mo>/</mo>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msup>
                                 <mo>,</mo>
                              </mrow>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> &lt;math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2503:cey2503-math-0015" display="block" wiley:location="equation/cey2503-math-0015.png" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mi&gt;i&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x0003D}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;k&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mn&gt;1&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;v&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mo&gt;\unicode{x0002B}&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;msub&gt;&lt;mi&gt;k&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/msub&gt;&lt;msup&gt;&lt;mi&gt;v&lt;/mi&gt;&lt;mrow&gt;&lt;mn&gt;1&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;mo&gt;/&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;mn&gt;2&lt;/mn&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/msup&gt;&lt;mo&gt;,&lt;/mo&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/mrow&gt;&lt;/math&gt;</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>i</i> can be described as a combination of capacitive process <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and diffusion-controlled insertion <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. Among them, the values of slope <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and intercept <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be obtained from a linear plot of <i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> versus <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. Therefore, the contribution ratio of diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled processes can be estimated.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0038_R_d76722569e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span></div>
            
            <p>Compared with the PHC electrode, the capacitance contribution ratio of FHCT<sub>2</sub> increases more significantly with the scan rate, as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0004">4H</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8D</a>. Especially, the capacitive contribution of FHCT<sub>2</sub> (95.7%) is higher than that of PHC (72.9%) at a scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. It can be attributed to the fact that F-doping increases the defects concentration on the hard carbon surface, which improves the adsorption capacity of sodium ions and promotes rapid reaction kinetics so that the FHCT<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibits excellent rate performance.</p>
            
            <p>In general, the insertion/extraction of sodium ions with larger radii in graphitic microcrystallites would affect the carbon interlayer spacing of the material, while the adsorption/desorption behavior on the surface would not. Therefore, the microstructure changes of the FHCT<sub>2</sub> electrode within different voltage ranges were verified by in situ XRD (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>). In the high-potential region, the position of the (002) diffraction peak is barely shifted, which means that the carbon layer spacing remains almost constant, consistent with the adsorption characteristics of sodium ions on the surface. As the discharge depth intensifies, the diffraction peak gradually evolves to the left, indicating that the carbon layer spacing increases, corresponding to the insertion behavior of sodium ions in graphitic microcrystallites.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0039_R_d76722569e1367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv Energy Mater">39</a></sup></span> It is worth noting that FHCT<sub>2</sub> differs from the conventional hard-carbon layer spacing, which undergoes concentrated and obvious changes; this also confirms that a large enough carbon layer spacing can accommodate the insertion of sodium ions, and thus the degree of carbon layer spacing changes is weakened.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cey2503-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cey2503-sec-0060-title">2.4 Theoretical calculations</h3>
            
            <p>To interpret the reaction mechanism of sodium-ion storage behavior, the differential charge density and adsorption abilities of different F-doping adsorption sites were investigated.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0040_R_d76722569e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon">40</a></sup></span> Figures&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0005">5A</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> show the simulation results of the adsorption capacity of sodium ions at different sites on F-free defective carbon and F-doped defect carbon, and the corresponding adsorption energies are listed in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>. The comparison of the adsorption energy of sodium ions on different active sites of F-doped defect carbon indicates that the A2 active site exhibits the highest adsorption energy (−3.00 eV), suggesting that it is the most stable sodium ion adsorption site on F-doped hard carbon. In addition, the adsorption energy of this site is higher than that of the most stable site on the defect carbon (−2.87 eV), suggesting that F-doping can facilitate the ion adsorption capacity.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0041_R_d76722569e1393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Nano">41</a></sup></span> This stronger surface site adsorption caused by F-doping favors the capacitive behavior of carbon material, especially showing a positive effect on improving the rate performance.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0042_R_d76722569e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J Mater Chem C">42</a></sup></span> As one of the halogens, F not only has the electron-withdrawing effect (electronegativity) but also exhibits the resonance donating effect (lone pair of electrons), which can change the electronic structure of the electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#cey2503-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2503-bib-0043_R_d76722569e1400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ Sci">43</a></sup></span> The differential charge densities around F-doped defective and F-free defective carbon sites are compared, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0005">5B</a>. The monovacancy created by the F–C bond transforms this site into an electron-deficient region with the tendency to accept electrons from Na. A large number of electrons accumulate around the monovacancy site of F-doped defective carbon, which causes a preference for the sodium ion to bind to the F-doping defect.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cey2503-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6bfa18ee-4bbb-4d74-b696-e2d8e59fbb26/cey2503-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>

            <p>Additionally, for the density of state (DOS), the F-doped defective carbon shows a reduced electronic state near the Fermi level (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2503-fig-0005">5C</a>), meaning that the conductivity is lower than that of Fe-free defective carbon, which is consistent with the actual conductivity measured by the four-probe method.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Ionic conductivity is a key factor determining the rate performance of hard carbon, which directly depends on the diffusion kinetics of sodium ions.
